Jaguar F-Type — Sections 1-7
Classification: Economics vs Risk
Justification: Performance-oriented, multi-generation (X152), buyers routinely weigh an expensive engine (AJ133/AJ126) against a depreciating sports car — the same tension as Range Rover Sport in the Land Rover precedent.

SECTION 4 — GENERATIONS — <GenerationsGrid>
H2: F-Type — One Generation, Three Powertrain Eras
Sub-headline: The X152 ran from 2013 to 2024 with one chassis but three distinct engine families — choose by reliability as much as soundtrack.

X152 (2013–2024) — The F-Type's only generation
Three distinct powertrain eras across a single chassis
2013–2019: Supercharged V6 & V8 Era
AJ126 3.0 V6 SC (340–380bhp)
AJ133 5.0 V8 SC (495–575bhp, SVR)
★ ★ ★ ☆ ☆ (pre-2019 cooling system caveats apply)
Our Verdict: The sound that made the F-Type famous. The AJ133 V8 is glorious — but pre-2019 cars need a cooling-system inspection before you buy, and the AJ126's plastic crossover pipes are a known weak point. Budget £1,000–£2,000 for cooling-system preventative work if history is unclear.

2019–2024: Revised V8 + Straight-Six Era
AJ133 5.0 V8 SC (facelift revisions, 450–575bhp)
AJ20D6 3.0 straight-six (P300/P340/P400)
★ ★ ★ ★ ★ (post-2019) / ★ ★ ★ ★ ☆ (revised V8)
Our Verdict: The car Jaguar should have built from the start. The AJ20D6 straight-six ditches the V6's cooling-system weak points for a clean reliability record, and the revised V8 carries over the 2013+ tensioner improvements. Buy a 2019+ car with the straight-six if reliability is your priority — you lose none of the character.

Confidence Score panel:
How confident are these ratings? AJ126, AJ133 and AJ20D6 ratings: High confidence — based on 560+ [JAG-VERIFIED] enquiries across the AJ126 and AJ133 engine families, cross-referenced with specialist interviews and documented failure patterns. Data note (internal):
Correction applied: The original data file listed 306DT (2.7/3.0 TDV6 diesel) as an F-Type engine. This was corrected → no diesel engine was ever fitted to the F-Type. Removed from engine list.
Correction applied: 508PN was initially listed as an F-Type engine code. Corrected → 508PN is the naturally aspirated 5.0 V8 (used in XK, XJ). The F-Type uses AJ133 (supercharged 5.0 V8), not 508PN. 508PN has been removed from the F-Type engine list.
AJ20D6 verification: Confirmed via live verification that the 3.0 straight-six Ingenium (AJ20D6) was introduced to the F-Type in 2019 (facelift) as the replacement for the AJ126 V6. Power outputs: P300 (300bhp), P340 (340bhp), P400 (400bhp). This is correctly attributed as the F-Type's late-generation engine.

SECTION 6 — COMMON PROBLEMS — <CommonProblems>
H2: Common F-Type Engine Problems
Sub-headline: The six most frequent F-Type failure patterns — what to check, which engines they affect, and what they cost.
#
Problem
Description
Severity
Link
1
AJ126 Cooling System Crossover Pipe Failure
The plastic coolant crossover pipes on the supercharged 3.0 V6 crack with age and heat cycling. These engines cannot tolerate overheating without warping heads or damaging the supercharger. Pre-2016 cars are highest risk.
🟠 Immediate
Read more →
2
AJ133 Timing Chain Tensioner Wear (Pre-2013)
The 5.0 V8's early-build weak point — chain tensioner design was revised in 2013. F-Type production started in 2013, so most F-Types are post-revision, but early 2013 cars without documented tensioner history need inspection.
🟡 Monitor
Read more →
3
Supercharger Coupler / Torsion Isolator Rattle
A well-known shared quirk of the AJ126 and AJ133 — the coupler between the supercharger and drive pulley wears, producing a rattling noise on idle. Rarely catastrophic on its own, but replacement involves supercharger removal.
🟡 Monitor
Read more →
4
AJ126 Water Pump Failure
The water pump on the supercharged V6 has a documented failure pattern — leaks or seizes without warning. Pre-2016 cars are most affected. Replacement cost: £600–£1,200 [JAG-QUOTE].
🟡 Monitor
Read more →
5
AJ133 Cooling System Crossover Pipe Leaks
Same plastic crossover pipe weakness as the AJ126, but on the 5.0 V8. Later (2016+) cars use revised parts. Inspect for coolant residue around the engine valley.
🟠 Immediate
Read more →
6
ZF 8-Speed Gearbox Mechatronic Issues
Shared across the JLR range — the 8HP's mechatronic unit can develop solenoid faults, causing harsh shifts or transmission faults. Not engine-specific but frequently enquired alongside engine problems.
🟡 Monitor
Read more →


Urgency key: 🔴 Catastrophic — stop driving, specialist inspection immediately • 🟠 Immediate — book inspection now • 🟡 Monitor — book inspection soon • 🟢 Low — plan at next service

Insights from the data:
Interesting insight: The F-Type's 53 enquiries in 2025 make it Jaguar's lowest-volume model in the dataset — but its enquiry-to-value ratio is among the highest, with average host values of £18,000–£65,000 making replacement economics unusually safe compared to lower-value Jags. 
The V6-to-straight-six transition is barely visible in the data. F-Type enquiries are overwhelmingly for the AJ126 V6 (560 across all models) — but this figure is aggregated across all AJ126-equipped Jags, not F-Type-specific. The AJ20D6 straight-six is too recent and too low-volume in F-Type fitment to generate meaningful proprietary data yet. 
The F-Type's cooling-system failure pattern dominates the conversation. Across AJ126 and AJ133 enquiries, cooling system (crossover pipes, water pumps) and timing-chain tensioner (pre-2013 units) are the most frequently mentioned symptoms. A buyer of any pre-2019 F-Type should budget £1,000–£2,000 for cooling-system preventative work unless full history shows it's been done.

SECTION 7B — EDITORIAL PULL-QUOTE — <EditorialPullQuote>
The One Thing We'd Tell Every F-Type Buyer
"Buy a 2019+ car with the AJ20D6 straight-six if you want the cleanest reliability record — it trades the V6's cooling-system worries for a modern turbocharged six with no systemic failure patterns. If you must have the V8, buy a 2016+ car with documented cooling-system history and budget £1,500–£2,000 for preventative work. The V6 sounds glorious, but pre-2019 cooling system maintenance is a 'when', not an 'if'."

Labour estimate: Fitting an F-Type engine typically takes 12–18 hours at £65–£95/hr (independent) or £110–£160/hr (London/SE dealer) [JAG-QUOTE].

The F-Type rule — economics vs soundtrack: An F-Type's typical value of £18,000–£65,000 means even the AJ133's upper reconditioned cost of £9,000 sits at 15–50% of vehicle value — well below our 60% threshold for Jaguar. The F-Type is one of the safest Jaguars to replace an engine on, purely because its retained values comfortably absorb the cost. The only caveat: if your F-Type is a high-mileage, early-register car worth £10,000–£15,000, a £9,000 AJ133 rebuild starts to approach 60–90% of vehicle value. In that scenario, evaluate the car's condition, history and mileage before committing — a low-mileage example is always worth saving; a tired one might not be.

SECTION 9 — HOW THE F-TYPE ENGINE RANGE EVOLVED — <EngineEvolution>
H2: How the F-Type Engine Range Evolved — 2013 to Today
Era
Years
Key Engines
Why Jaguar Changed
Worth Knowing
Launch Era — Supercharged V6 & V8
2013–2015
AJ126 3.0 V6 SC (340/380), AJ133 5.0 V8 SC (450/550), SVR (575)
Jaguar needed a sports car to follow the XK — the F-Type launched with the AJ-V8 family already well-established from the XK and XJ.
The AJ126 is essentially an AJ133 V8 with two cylinders removed — same architecture, same supercharger layout, same cooling-system design.
Facelift Era — Straight-Six Arrives
2019–2020
AJ20D6 3.0 I6 Turbo (P300/P340/P400), AJ133 5.0 V8 SC (revised)
JLR's Ingenium straight-six replaced the AJ126 V6 across the range — emissions, efficiency, and a clean-sheet design with no cooling-system weaknesses.
The AJ20D6 is the same engine family as the Defender's D300 and Range Rover's P400 — a modular straight-six with no systemic failure patterns.
Final Era — Electrification Prep
2020–2024
AJ20D6, AJ133 V8
Jaguar's transition to all-electric by 2025 meant the F-Type's days were numbered — the V8 carried on in reduced volumes for enthusiasts.
The F-Type was discontinued in 2024 with no direct successor — the last of Jaguar's internal-combustion sports cars.


SECTION 10 — WHO SHOULD BUY AN F-TYPE? — <WhoShouldBuy>
H2: Who Should Buy an F-Type?
Buyer Profile
Rating
Our Verdict
Performance Buyer
★★★★★
The F-Type's supercharged V8 delivers one of the best sports-car soundtracks of any modern car. The 575bhp SVR is the one to hunt — but budget £1,500–£2,000 for cooling-system preventative work on any pre-2019 car.
Weekend Enthusiast
★★★★☆
The F-Type is at its best on a Sunday B-road. The V6 is sweeter than the V8 for pure handling balance — the 380bhp V6 is the enthusiast's sweet spot if you can live with the cooling-system caveats.
Daily Commuter
★★★☆☆
The F-Type is a sports car, not a grand tourer. The ride is firm, the boot is small, and the running costs are real. If you're commuting daily in an F-Type, buy a 2019+ car with the AJ20D6 — it's the most civilised and the most reliable.
Collector / Keeper
★★★★☆
The F-Type is the last of Jaguar's internal-combustion sports cars — that alone makes it a future classic. A low-mileage, early-register V8 SVR with full history is the long-term keeper. The AJ20D6 cars are better to own and drive, but the V8 will be the collector's choice.
First-Time Sports Car Buyer
★★★★☆
The F-Type is a surprisingly approachable sports car — the V6 is manageable, the chassis is forgiving, and the drama is instantly addictive. Buy a 2019+ car with the AJ20D6: no cooling-system worries, modern infotainment, and the cleanest reliability record in the range.


SECTION 11 — REPAIR OR REPLACE? — <CalculatorCTA>
H2: Can I Repair My F-Type Engine — or Should I Replace It?
The F-Type's AJ126 V6 cooling-system failures are the most common repair — if the plastic crossover pipes have cracked and you've caught it early, a repair is usually viable. If it has overheated and warped the heads, replacement is often more cost-effective. The AJ133 V8 follows the same logic — cooling system repairs are routine, but a full rebuild is serious money.

SECTION 13 — F-TYPE FAQ — <FAQAccordion>
H2: F-Type Frequently Asked Questions
Q1: How much does an F-Type engine replacement cost in the UK?
An F-Type engine replacement typically costs £4,500–£9,000 for a reconditioned engine, supply-only [JAG-QUOTE]. Labour adds 12–18 hours at £65–£95/hr (independent) or £110–£160/hr (London/SE dealer). The AJ126 V6 sits at the lower end (£4,500–£7,500), while the AJ133 V8 sits higher (£5,500–£9,000). The AJ20D6 straight-six sits in the middle (£4,500–£7,500). Use our diagnostic calculator above to get a personalised quote for your specific car.
Q2: Which F-Type engine is the most reliable?
The AJ20D6 3.0 straight-six (fitted to 2019+ P300/P340/P400 cars) is the most reliable F-Type engine. It has no systemic cooling-system weaknesses, no chain-tensioner caveats, and no supercharger-coupler wear issues — it's a clean-sheet design that has proven robust across the JLR range. Read our AJ20D6 Knowledge Centre → for the full breakdown.
Q3: Which F-Type engine should I avoid?
No engine outright — but the AJ126 3.0 V6 SC (2013–2019) carries a real caveat: pre-2019 cars have documented cooling-system crossover pipe failures. If you're buying an early V6, insist on evidence that the cooling system has been inspected and the pipes replaced if needed. The AJ133 5.0 V8 is robust post-2013, but cooling-system maintenance is still a "when", not an "if".
Q4: Is the F-Type timing chain problem real?
The timing chain tensioner issue affects the AJ133 5.0 V8 built before 2013 — the design was revised in 2013 with a marked reliability improvement. F-Type production started in 2013, so most F-Types are post-revision, but early 2013 cars without documented chain-tensioner history warrant an inspection. Symptoms include a rattle on cold start — if you hear it, have a specialist check it immediately.
Q5: Is it worth replacing an F-Type engine?
Yes — almost always. The F-Type's typical value of £18,000–£65,000 means even the AJ133's upper reconditioned cost of £9,000 sits at 15–50% of vehicle value, well below our 60% threshold for Jaguar. The only exception is a high-mileage, early-register car worth £10,000–£15,000 where a £9,000 V8 rebuild starts to approach 60–90% of value. In that scenario, evaluate the car's condition and history before committing.
Q6: Which F-Type year should I buy?
Buy a 2019+ car with the AJ20D6 straight-six if your priority is reliability and modern engineering. Buy a 2013–2019 V6 if the supercharged soundtrack is non-negotiable — but budget £1,000–£2,000 for cooling-system preventative work unless history shows it's been done. Buy the V8 if you want the full-fat experience — the post-2013 tensioner revisions make it robust, but cooling-system maintenance is essential.
Q7: How do I find my F-Type engine code?
Your F-Type's engine code is on the VIN plate (character 4–8), stamped on the engine block (passenger side, rear), and on the V5C registration document. The codes are: AJ126 (3.0 V6 SC, 2013–2019), AJ133 (5.0 V8 SC, 2013–2024), and AJ20D6 (3.0 straight-six, 2019–2024). If you're unsure, use our identification tool → Identify My Engine →
Q8: F-Type vs XK — which is more reliable?
The F-Type's AJ133 V8 is the direct descendant of the XK's AJ133 — the F-Type benefits from the 2013+ timing-chain revisions that transformed the V8's reliability record. The XK carries the pre-2013 risk on its 5.0 cars. For V6 buyers, the F-Type's AJ126 is the same engine as the XK's later V6 — identical cooling-system caveats. The F-Type wins on reliability purely because of age: it's a newer car with more recent engineering. Read our full F-Type vs XK comparison →

Classification: Premium Style / Widest Economics Swing
Justification: The XK spans two distinct generations (X100 1996–2006, X150 2006–2014) with a wide value range from sub-£5,000 early XK8s to £30,000+ low-mileage XKR-S models, and shares its AJ-V8 engine family with multiple other Jaguar models — replacement economics depend entirely on which specific car you own.

SECTION 4 — GENERATIONS — <GenerationsGrid>
H2: XK Generations — Find Your Vehicle

X100 (1996–2006) — 🔥 Most Enquired — 120 enquiries (2025) [JAG-VERIFIED]
1996–2006 • 4.0 AJ27 V8 • 4.2 AJ34 V8
★★★★☆
Our Verdict: The original modern XK — beautiful, characterful, and increasingly collectable. Buy a 2003+ 4.2 car with full history; early 4.0 cars need bore-wear checks and timing chain inspection before purchase.
Explore X100 →

X150 (2006–2014)
2006–2014 • 4.2 AJ34 V8 • 5.0 AJ133 V8 • 5.0 AJ133 Supercharged V8
★★★★★
Our Verdict: The definitive XK — Ian Callum's aluminium masterpiece. The 2009 facelift brought the 5.0 V8 with much-improved timing chain design. This is the one to buy, especially the naturally aspirated 5.0 Portfolio or the thunderous XKR-S.
Explore X150 →

The 2009 Facelift — The Dividing Line
Pre-2009 (4.2-litre): The original X150 launched in 2006 with the 4.2 AJ34 V8 — a proven unit, but the 5.0 that replaced it is objectively better in every way.
2009–2014 (5.0-litre): The 5.0 AJ133 brought more power (385bhp naturally aspirated, 503–542bhp supercharged), a revised timing chain tensioner design, and a subtle styling refresh. This is the generation every informed buyer targets.
Read the full comparison: X100 vs X150 →

How confident are these ratings? AJ133 and AJ133S ratings: 85% confidence — based on 330 [JAG-VERIFIED] enquiries plus cross-referencing with specialist and owner-forum sources. Data note (internal):
Correction applied: The source data file lists "508PS" as an engine code for the XK. 508PS is the marketing power designation for the 5.0 supercharged V8; the factory engine code is AJ133 (with supercharged variants carrying the same base code). The home Jaguar homepage correctly identifies AJ133 / 508PN as the 5.0 V8 family.
Correction applied: The source data file groups all AJ133 enquiries (330) under a single code. This figure includes XF and XJ enquiries as well as XK — it is not XK-exclusive. The XK-specific enquiry volume for 5.0-litre cars is lower; the 330 figure is cited in the engine table as the total for the engine family, with the model-level XK figure (120 for X100 + approx 170 for X150 = 290+) used in the hero and trust strip.

SECTION 6 — COMMON PROBLEMS — <CommonProblems>
H2: Common XK Engine Problems
Sub-headline: What actually fails on the XK's AJ-V8 engines — and what it costs to fix.
#
Problem
Description
Severity
Link
1
AJ27 4.0 V8 Bore Wear
Early X100 engines (pre-2000) can suffer cylinder bore wear, causing misfires and blue exhaust smoke on throttle. Full engine rebuild is the only permanent fix.
🟡 Monitor
Read more →
2
AJ-V8 Timing Chain Tensioner Wear
Pre-2009 AJ27 and early AJ34 engines have a documented timing chain tensioner weakness. Cold-start rattle is the early warning sign — catastrophic if ignored.
🟡 Monitor
Read more →
3
AJ133 5.0 V8 Water Pump Failure
The 5.0 V8's water pump is a known weak point across the AJ133 family. Owner reports confirm it's a routine replacement item — catch it before overheating causes head-gasket damage.
🟠 Immediate
Read more →
4
ZF 6-Speed Gearbox Torque Converter
Shared across the X150 range — torque converter lock-up clutch failure causes gearbox slippage, grinding noises and transmission warning lights.
🟡 Monitor
Read more →
5
Battery Drain & Electrical Gremlins
XKs are sensitive to battery condition — modules failing to sleep correctly is a frequent no-start complaint. Always use a premium battery.
🟢 Low
Read more →
6
Fuel Injector Failure (5.0 V8)
Faulty injectors on the 5.0 AJ133 cause rough running, reduced power, stalling and excessive fuel consumption.
🟢 Low
Read more →

Urgency key: 🔴 Catastrophic — stop driving, specialist inspection immediately • 🟠 Immediate — book inspection now • 🟡 Monitor — book inspection soon • 🟢 Low — plan at next service

Insights from the data
Interesting insight: The X150 generation accounts for roughly 60% of all XK enquiries in 2025 — the aluminium grand tourer is the one buyers are actively researching, while the X100 is increasingly the preserve of collectors and enthusiasts 
The 5.0 AJ133 engine (fitted to 2009–2014 X150s) generates significantly more enquiry volume than any other XK engine — buyers are actively seeking the post-facelift car, and specialists report this is the generation with the strongest reliability record 
Early 4.0 X100 cars generate relatively low enquiry volume but high forum activity — the enthusiast community is active, but the cars are now cheap enough that a full engine replacement often fails the economics threshold for all but the best examples 

SECTION 7B — EDITORIAL PULL-QUOTE — <EditorialPullQuote>
The One Thing We'd Tell Every XK Buyer
Buy a 2009–2014 X150 with the 5.0 naturally aspirated V8, full service history, and evidence the water pump has been replaced. It's the sweet spot — the most reliable engine, the best-looking generation, and values that are still £10,000–£20,000 below where they should be.

Labour estimate: Fitting an XK engine typically takes 12–18 hours at £65–£95/hr (independent) or £110–£160/hr (London/SE dealer) [JAG-QUOTE].

The XK rule — the value swing is the story: The XK's value range is one of the widest in the Jaguar line-up. An early X100 XK8 worth £4,000–£5,000 facing a £5,000+ reconditioned engine sits at 100%+ of vehicle value — well above our 60% scrap threshold for Jaguar. The same engine in a 2014 XKR-S worth £30,000–£35,000 sits at just 20–30% of vehicle value — a no-brainer replacement. Before committing to a recon engine, get a realistic market valuation for your specific car and run the numbers. For X100s, used engines often make more sense; for X150s, a reconditioned unit is usually the right call.

SECTION 9 — HOW THE XK ENGINE RANGE EVOLVED — <EngineEvolution>
H2: How the XK Engine Range Evolved — 1996 to 2014
Era
Years
Key Engines
Why Jaguar Changed
Worth Knowing
The AJ27 Era
1996–2002
AJ27 4.0 V8, AJ27S 4.0 Supercharged
The original XK8 launched with Jaguar's new 4.0-litre V8 — a clean-sheet design replacing the straight-six XJS.
Early cars (pre-2000) have a reputation for bore wear — always inspect before buying.
The 4.2 Upgrade
2002–2006
AJ34 4.2 V8, AJ34S 4.2 Supercharged
Jaguar increased displacement to 4.2 litres in 2002, bringing more power and improved reliability — the bore-wear issues were largely resolved.
The 4.2 is significantly more robust than the early 4.0 — this is the X100 to target.
The Aluminium Revolution
2006–2009
AJ34 4.2 V8, AJ34S 4.2 Supercharged
The X150 launched with an aluminium monocoque, losing 150kg over the X100 — the 4.2 engine carried over initially.
The X150 chassis is a masterpiece — Ian Callum's finest work.
The 5.0 Transformation
2009–2014
AJ133 5.0 V8, AJ133S 5.0 Supercharged
The 2009 facelift brought the 5.0-litre AJ133 V8 with 385bhp (naturally aspirated) and up to 542bhp (supercharged) — plus a revised timing chain tensioner design.
This is the definitive XK engine — more power, stronger reliability, and the one specialists recommend.


SECTION 10 — WHO SHOULD BUY AN XK? — <WhoShouldBuy>
H2: Who Should Buy an XK?
Buyer Profile
Rating
Our Verdict
Performance Buyer
★★★★★
The XKR-S is one of the most underrated performance cars on the used market — 542bhp, 200mph+, and values that haven't yet caught up to the driving experience. Buy a 2012+ car with full service history.
Daily Commuter
★★★☆☆
The XK is a genuine GT — comfortable, refined, and capable of daily use. But be realistic: fuel economy is 18–25mpg, and maintenance costs reflect the original £70,000 price tag. A 2009+ 5.0 naturally aspirated is the sweet spot.
Family Buyer
★★☆☆☆
The XK is a 2+2 at best — rear seats are tiny, and even children will complain. This is a car for couples, not families. If you need practicality, look at the F-Pace instead.
Budget Owner
★★☆☆☆
An early X100 can be bought for under £5,000 — but a £5,000 engine replacement will quickly exceed the car's value. Buy the best X150 5.0 you can afford, not the cheapest X100 you can find.
Collector / Keeper
★★★★★
The XK, especially the X150, is a future classic. Low-mileage examples with full service history are already appreciating. The XKR-S and Portfolio models are the ones to hold long-term.


SECTION 11 — REPAIR OR REPLACE? — <CalculatorCTA>
H2: Can I Repair My XK Engine — or Should I Replace It?
The XK presents one of the widest economics swings in the Jaguar range — a £5,000 reconditioned engine in a £4,000 X100 is a financial mistake, but the same engine in a £30,000 XKR-S is a no-brainer. Run the numbers before you commit.

SECTION 13 — XK FAQ — <FAQAccordion>
H2: XK Frequently Asked Questions
Q1: How much does an XK engine replacement cost in the UK?
An XK engine replacement costs between £3,000 and £10,000 supply-only depending on engine choice — the 4.0 AJ27 sits at the lower end (£3,000–£5,500), while the 5.0 AJ133 supercharged sits at the top (£6,500–£10,000) [JAG-QUOTE]. Labour adds approximately 12–18 hours at £65–£95/hr for an independent specialist. Use our diagnostic calculator to get a personalised estimate for your specific XK.
Q2: Which XK engine is the most reliable?
The AJ133 5.0 V8 (2009–2014) is the most reliable XK engine. The 2009 facelift brought a revised timing chain tensioner design that resolved the weakness of earlier AJ-V8s, and the 5.0-litre unit has proven robust across the X150 range. The naturally aspirated 385bhp version is the sweet spot — simpler than the supercharged variant with fewer potential failure points.
Q3: Which XK engine should I avoid?
Avoid the early AJ27 4.0 V8 (1996–2000) without documented history — these engines suffer from bore wear (blue smoke on throttle, misfires) and timing chain tensioner issues. If you're set on an X100, buy a 2003+ 4.2 car — the AJ34 is significantly more robust. For the X150, all engines are solid, but prioritise a 2009+ 5.0 car for the best reliability.
Q4: Does the XK have timing chain problems?
Yes — pre-2009 AJ-V8 engines (AJ27, early AJ34) have a documented timing chain tensioner weakness. Cold-start rattle is the early warning sign. If you hear it, book an inspection immediately — ignoring it can cause catastrophic engine failure. Post-2009 5.0 AJ133 engines have a revised design and are much more robust.
Q5: Is it worth replacing an XK engine?
It depends entirely on the car's value. A 2014 XKR-S worth £30,000–£35,000 with a £6,500–£10,000 recon cost sits at 20–30% of vehicle value — absolutely worth replacing. A 1998 XK8 worth £4,000–£5,000 facing a £5,000+ recon cost sits at over 100% of vehicle value — not worth it. If replacement cost exceeds roughly 60% of your car's market value, get a scrap valuation before committing. Use our diagnostic calculator for a personalised assessment.
Q6: Which XK should I buy — X100 or X150?
Buy an X150 (2009–2014) with the 5.0 naturally aspirated V8 — it's the best-looking generation, the most reliable engine, and values are still £10,000–£20,000, which is remarkably low for a car of this quality. The X100 is a beautiful classic but the economics are tighter — only buy one if you're prepared for classic-car ownership costs.
Q7: How do I find my XK engine code?
Your XK's engine code can be found in three places: (1) on the VIN plate inside the driver's door sill — the engine code is part of the VIN; (2) on the engine block itself — stamped on the front of the AJ-V8 block; (3) in your service documentation or V5C logbook. Our 15+ vetted Jaguar specialists can also identify your engine if you're unsure.
Q8: XK vs XKR — which is more reliable?
Both use the same AJ-V8 engine family — the XKR simply adds a supercharger. The naturally aspirated XK has fewer components to fail (no supercharger, no intercooler), so it's technically the more reliable option. However, the XKR's supercharged V8 is robust when maintained — 2009+ examples with the 5.0 AJ133S have proven very reliable. Our honest advice: buy the XKR if you want the performance; the reliability difference is minor. Read the full comparison: XK vs XKR →
